Disbelief and Faith After the Covenant
Commentary on two matn statements about disbelief and faith after the covenant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between innate covenantal faith and acquired disbelief, and the requirement for verbal faith to align with heart's affirmation.

Core Idea
Innate faith can be lost by deliberate disbelief but confirmed by sincere manifestation.

This passage from the commentary explains the fate of those who disbelieve or believe after the primal covenant. It clarifies that disbelieving after the covenant means changing one's innate God-given faith, while true faith requires harmony between the tongue and the heart. We'll explore these two outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms and the structure of the commentary.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: one who disbelieves after the covenant, and one who manifests faith truly. Note how each is described.
3
Understand the key terms
Focus on the terms: الإيمان الميثاقي, الفطري الوهبي, الكسبي, اللساني, and تصديق الجنان. How do they relate to the two scenarios?
These terms distinguish innate, acquired, and manifested faith.
4
Reflect on the condition for confirmation
The passage says verbal faith must correspond to heart's affirmation. Why is this important? Also note the emphasis on persistence (wa damma/wa dawama).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن كفر بعد ذلك أي الإيمان الميثاقي فقد بدّل وغيّر أي إيمانه الفطري الوهبي بالفكر الطارئ الكسبي ومن أظهر إيمانه وصدق أي في إظهاره بأن يكون إيمانه اللساني مطابقا لتصديق الجنان فقد ثبت عليه أي على دينه كما في نسخة، والمعنى على دينه الأصلي وفطرته الأولى ودام أي على الإسلام، وهو تأكيد لما قبله، وفي نسخة: وداوم أي واستمر عليه ولم يتزلزل لديه.
And whoever disbelieves after that, meaning the covenantal faith, has certainly altered and changed, meaning his innate God-given faith with acquired accidental thought. And whoever manifests his faith and believes truly, meaning in his manifestation such that his verbal faith corresponds to the affirmation of the heart, has certainly confirmed it, meaning his religion, as in one copy, and the meaning is upon his original religion and his first innate disposition, and continues, meaning upon Islam, and it is an emphasis of what preceded. And in another copy: 'and continued' meaning he persisted upon it and did not waver.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two statements from the matn (original text) concerning the fate of those who disbelieve or believe after the making of the covenant. The first statement: 'Whoever disbelieves after that' refers to disbelief after the covenantal faith (al-iman al-mithaqi). Such a person has altered and changed his innate God-given faith (al-iman al-fitri al-wahbi) with acquired accidental thought (al-fikr al-tari' al-kasbi). The second statement: 'Whoever manifests his faith and believes truly' means that his verbal profession (iman lisani) corresponds to the affirmation of the heart (tasdiq al-janan). Such a person has confirmed his religion—his original religion and his first innate disposition (fitratuhu al-ula). The word 'wa damma' (and continues) is emphasized from what preceded, and in another version 'wa dawama' (and persisted) meaning he remained steadfast without wavering. Thus, the passage distinguishes between the one who changes his innate faith through disbelief and the one who confirms it through sincere faith.

Key Terms
الإيمان الميثاقي
الفطري الوهبي
الكسبي
اللساني
تصديق الجنان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the covenantal faith alone suffices and no further action is needed.
Correction: The passage shows that acquired disbelief can change innate faith, and true faith requires deliberate manifestation with harmony between tongue and heart, and persistence.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does 'wa damma' (and continues) emphasize?
A) The person's worldly life
B) His persistence upon Islam without wavering
C) The covenant itself
D) The intellect

Key Takeaway
After the covenant, disbelief changes innate faith, while sincere faith requires verbal and heart harmony and persistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself saying words of faith while your heart felt distant or doubtful? What does this passage reveal about the gap between your tongue and your heart, and what will you do today to close it?
?
Think of a moment when your beliefs were tested and you wavered. According to this lesson, that wavering is not trivial—it touches your innate covenant. What is one area where you need to stop wavering and commit to steadfastness starting now?
?
If you were to honestly assess your daily life, is your faith something you actively confirm through both speech and inner conviction, or have you been relying on a passive 'covenantal faith' from birth? What step will you take to manifest your faith truly before you sleep tonight?

Three Categories of People and Their Divine Commands
Imam Abu Hanifa classifies people into three types and assigns each a specific divine command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of people according to Imam Abu Hanifa and the specific divine command directed to each.

Core Idea
Allah has a unique command for each type of person: action, faith, or sincerity.

Imam Abu Hanifa presents a foundational classification of humanity into three spiritual states: the sincere believer, the denying disbeliever, and the dissembling hypocrite. Each state carries a distinct obligation from Allah, as revealed in the Qur'anic address to 'mankind.' This passage clarifies that divine commands are not one-size-fits-all; they target the specific condition of each group.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the preamble
Read the opening lines affirming Allah as Creator and Provider, and the ruling on lawful earning. This sets the theological foundation.
The verse from Ar-Rum emphasizes Allah's sole power.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three types: the sincere believer, the denying disbeliever, and the dissembling hypocrite. Note the specific terms used.
Each category has a qualifier: mukhlis, jahid, mudahin.
3
Connect each category to its obligation
Note what Allah obligated on each: action for the believer, faith for the disbeliever, sincerity for the hypocrite. Understand why each is appropriate.
This challenges the assumption that a single command covers all.
4
Examine the Qur'anic evidence
Read verse 2:21 and how Imam Abu Hanifa interprets 'O mankind' as addressing each group differently. Reflect on the depth of this reading.
The same verse carries layered meanings for different audiences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الإمام الأعظم في كتابه "الوصية": ثم نقر بأن الله تعالى خالق الخلق ورازقهم، ولم يكن لهم طاقة؛ لأنهم ضعفاء عاجزون محدثون، والله تعالى خالقهم ورازقهم لقوله سبحانه: ﴿ اللهُ الَّذِي خَلَقَكُمْ ثُمَّ رَزَقَكُمْ ثُمَّ يُمِيتُكُمْ ثُمَّ يُحْيِيكُمْ ﴾ [الروم: ٤٠]، والكسب من الحلال حلال، وجمع المال من الحرام حرام، والخلق على ثلاثة أصناف: المؤمن المخلص في إيمانه، والكافر الجاحد في كفره، والمنافق المداهن في نفاقه، والله تعالى فرض على المؤمن العمل وعلى الكافر الإيمان وعلى المنافق الإخلاص بقوله تعالى: ﴿ يَا أَيُّهَا النَّاسُ اعْبُدُوا رَبَّكُمُ الَّذِي خَلَقَكُمْ ﴾ [البقرة: ٢١]، ومعناه: يا أيها المؤمنون أطيعوا الله، ويا أيها الكافرون آمنوا بالله، ويا أيها المنافقون أخلصوا الله. انتهى.
And the Greatest Imam said in his book 'Al-Wasiyya': Then we affirm that Allah, Exalted is He, is the Creator of creation and their Provider, and they have no power because they are weak, incapable, and originated. Allah, Exalted is He, is their Creator and Provider due to His statement, Exalted is He: { Allah is the One who created you, then provided for you, then causes you to die, then gives you life } [Ar-Rum: 40]. And earning from what is lawful is lawful, and accumulating wealth from what is unlawful is unlawful. And creation are of three types: the believer who is sincere in his faith, the disbeliever who denies in his disbelief, and the hypocrite who dissembles in his hypocrisy. And Allah, Exalted is He, obligated upon the believer action, upon the disbeliever faith, and upon the hypocrite sincerity, by His statement, Exalted is He: { O mankind, worship your Lord who created you } [Al-Baqara: 21]. Its meaning: O believers, obey Allah; O disbelievers, believe in Allah; O hypocrites, be sincere to Allah. End.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage reaffirms from Imam Abu Hanifa that Allah is the sole Creator and Provider. Humans have no power of their own; they are weak and originated (muhdath). The verse from Ar-Rum (40) is cited. Then the author notes the ruling on lawful and unlawful earning. People are classified into three types: the sincere believer, the denying disbeliever, and the dissembling hypocrite. Each has a specific obligation from Allah: the believer must perform actions (amal), the disbeliever must have faith (iman), and the hypocrite must be sincere (ikhlas). The verse 'O mankind, worship your Lord' (Al-Baqara: 21) is interpreted to address each group differently: believers are commanded to obey, disbelievers to believe, and hypocrites to be sincere.

Key Terms
مؤمن (mu'min / believer)
كافر (kafir / disbeliever)
منافق (munafiq / hypocrite)
إخلاص (ikhlas / sincerity)
عمل (amal / action)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine commands are identical for every person regardless of their spiritual state.
Correction: Imam Abu Hanifa shows that Allah addresses believers, disbelievers, and hypocrites with distinct obligations, as seen in the interpretation of 'O mankind, worship your Lord.'

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is obligated upon the hypocrite?
A) Action
B) Faith
C) Sincerity
D) Wealth

Key Takeaway
Allah's commands are tailored to the specific spiritual condition of each group: believers must act, disbelievers must believe, and hypocrites must be sincere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the verse 'O mankind, worship your Lord,' do you hear it as a generic address, or do you feel the specific weight of what Allah is commanding you personally? What is the one command you know you are neglecting right now?
?
Look at the three categories: believer, disbeliever, hypocrite. Is it possible that you are closest to the hypocrite—outwardly practicing but inwardly distant? What would it cost you to truly be sincere, and are you willing to pay that price today?
?
Allah commands the hypocrite to be sincere. Sincerity demands that you face your hidden faults. What is the secret you have been hiding from yourself and from Allah? Write it down, then pray for the courage to purify it.

Definitions of Major Sins
The author presents two definitions of major sins and gives his preferred view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main definitions of major sins among early scholars and why the Quran-based definition is considered the most apparent.

Core Idea
A major sin is what the Quran threatens with punishment.

Before we can classify sins, we need a clear definition of 'major sin'. Early scholars differed: some said every forbidden act is major, while others tied it to Quranic threats. The author presents both views and indicates the stronger one. This foundational discussion sets the stage for understanding Islamic jurisprudence on sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on definitions
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two opposing views.
Ibn Sirin's view is broader; the other scholars' view is narrower.
2
Identify the two definitions
Identify Ibn Sirin's definition (every forbidden act) and the definition of al-Hasan et al. (linked to threat of punishment).
3
Understand the author's preference
The author declares the Quran-linked definition as 'al-azhar' (most apparent). Reflect on why this might be stronger.
The verse itself uses the word 'kabair' for great sins, not all sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في حد الكبيرة، فقال ابن سيرين رضي الله عنه : كل ما نهى الله عنه فهو كبيرة، ويؤيده ظاهر قوله سبحانه وَإِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَائِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ الآية [النساء: ٣١]، وقال الحسن وسعيد بن جبير والضحاك وغيرهم ما جاء في القرآن مقرونا بذكر الوعيد فهو كبيرة، وهذا هو الأظهر فتدبر. ثم اعلم أن ترك الفرض أو الواجب ولو مرّة بلا عذر كبيرة وكذا ارتكاب الحرام. وترك السنة مرّة بلا عذر تساهلا وتكاسلا عنها صغيرة، وكذا ارتكاب الكراهة والإصرار على السنة أو ارتكاب الكراهة كبيرة، إلا أنها كبيرة دون كبيرة؛ لأن الكبير والصغير من الأمور الإضافية والأحوال النسبية ولذا قيل: حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين. قال شارح عقيدة الطحاوي وثم أمر ينبغي التفطن له وهو أن الكبيرة قد يقترن بها من الحياء والخوف والاستعظام لها ما يلحقها بالصغائر. وقد يقترن بالصغيرة من قلة الحياء وعدم المبالات وترك الخوف والاستهانة بها ما يلحقها بالكبائر، وهذا أمر مرجعه إلى ما يقوم بالقلب وهو قدر زائد على مجرد الفعل والإنسان يعرف ذلك من نفسه وغيره. وأيضاً فإنه قد يعفى لصاحب الإحسان العظيم ما لا يعفى لغيره من الذنب الجسيم. ثم هذه العصمة ثابتة للأنبياء قبل النبوة وبعدها على الأصح، وهم مؤيدون بالمعجزات الباهرات والآيات الظاهرات.
And they disagreed concerning the definition of the major sin. Ibn Sirin, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'Everything that Allah has forbidden is a major sin,' and this is supported by the apparent meaning of His statement, glorified be He: 'If you avoid the great sins which you are forbidden' [4:31]. And al-Hasan, Sa'id ibn Jubayr, al-Dahhak, and others said: 'What occurs in the Quran connected with a threat of punishment is a major sin,' and this is the most apparent view, so reflect. Then know that abandoning an obligatory or required act, even once without excuse, is a major sin, and likewise committing a forbidden act. And abandoning a sunna once without excuse out of leniency and laziness is a minor sin, and likewise committing a disliked act, while persisting in a sunna or committing a disliked act is a major sin, except that it is a lesser major sin; because the major and minor are relative matters and relational states, hence it is said: 'The good deeds of the righteous are the sins of those brought near.' The commentator of Aqidat al-Tahawi said: 'Then there is a matter that ought to be paid attention to: that a major sin may be accompanied by such shame, fear, and awe as to make it resemble minor sins; and a minor sin may be accompanied by such lack of shame, indifference, absence of fear, and contempt as to make it resemble major sins. This matter traces back to what is in the heart—an extra dimension beyond the mere act—and a person knows this from himself and from others. Moreover, for the possessor of great excellence, what is not forgiven for others from a grave sin may be forgiven.' Then this infallibility is established for the prophets before prophethood and after it according to the sounder view, and they are supported by dazzling miracles and manifest signs.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a theological discussion on the definition and nature of major sins (kabair). The author first presents differing opinions among early scholars. Ibn Sirin held that every forbidden act is major, citing the Quranic verse about avoiding 'the great sins which you are forbidden'. Others, like al-Hasan and Sa'id ibn Jubayr, defined a major sin as that which is connected in the Quran to a threat of punishment (al-wa'id), and the author notes this is the most apparent view, calling the reader to ponder. The author then provides practical rulings: abandoning an obligatory act (fard or wajib) once without excuse is major; committing a forbidden act is major; abandoning a sunna out of carelessness is minor; committing a disliked act is minor, but persisting in it or persisting in abandoning a sunna becomes major, albeit a lesser major sin. This leads to the principle that 'major' and 'minor' are relative (amur idafiyya), illustrated by the saying: 'The good deeds of the righteous are the sins of those brought near (to Allah).' The commentary from Sharh Aqidat al-Tahawi elaborates: a major sin can be 'downgraded' if accompanied by repentance, awe, etc., while a minor sin can be 'upgraded' if accompanied by indifference and contempt. This relativity is rooted in the heart's state, an extra dimension beyond the act itself. Finally, the author reaffirms that prophetic infallibility covers both before and after prophethood (the sounder view), and prophets are supported by miracles.

Key Terms
الكبيرة
الوعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think any sin is automatically a major sin regardless of context.
Correction: The passage shows that scholars defined major sins specifically as those connected to a threat of punishment in the Quran, not every forbidden act.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which definition of major sin does the author consider the most apparent?
A) Everything Allah forbids
B) What occurs in the Quran connected with a threat of punishment
C) What is mentioned in the Quran
D) What is prohibited by the Prophet

Key Takeaway
The most apparent definition of a major sin is that which is accompanied by a threat of punishment in the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin that you consider 'small,' do you reflect on whether it is accompanied by a threat of punishment in the Quran? How does this lesson challenge your casual attitude toward minor sins?
?
The author ends with 'so reflect' (فتدبر). Have you ever pondered the classification of your own sins? Will you now take time to identify which sins you commit that are explicitly threatened with punishment?
?
If the most apparent view is that a major sin is defined by a Quranic threat, what does that imply about the gravity of sins that are only mentioned without a threat? Are you comfortable ignoring them?

Rulings: When Is a Sin Major or Minor?
The author classifies sins according to the type of act and the presence of persistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the type of religious duty (fard, wajib, sunna, etc.) and persistence affect whether a sin is classified as major or minor.

Core Idea
Persistence can turn a minor sin into a major one.

After defining major sins, the author provides practical examples. He explains that abandoning an obligatory act even once is major, while abandoning a sunna is minor, but persisting in it elevates it to major. This nuanced classification teaches us that the same act can have different rulings depending on context and continuity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on rulings
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the list of acts: fard, wajib, sunna, makruh.
2
Identify the categories
For each act, note whether it is major or minor without persistence.
Fard/wajib: major; sunna/makruh: minor.
3
Understand the role of persistence
Notice that persistence (israr) in a sunna or makruh makes it major, but a 'lesser major' (kabira duna kabira).
This shows degrees within major sins.

Source Text

The Relativity of Sins and Prophetic Infallibility
The author explains the relativity of major and minor sins and concludes with prophetic infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the classification of sins as major or minor is relative to the person's state of heart and spiritual station, and that prophets are infallible both before and after prophethood.

Core Idea
The same sin can be major for one person and minor for another.

The author deepens our understanding by showing that 'major' and 'minor' are relative terms. The saying 'The good deeds of the righteous are the sins of those brought near' captures this. Then, through a commentary, he explains that the heart's state—shame, fear, indifference—can transform a sin's gravity. Finally, he affirms prophetic infallibility and miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on relativity
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the saying 'Hasanat al-abrar sayyiat al-muqarrabin'.
This saying is key to understanding relativity.
2
Understand the heart's role
In the commentary, note how shame and fear can downgrade a major sin, while indifference can upgrade a minor sin.
The heart's state is an extra dimension beyond the act.
3
Note the forgiveness for the excellent
The passage mentions that for those with great excellence, what is not forgiven for others may be forgiven.
4
Learn about prophetic infallibility
The passage concludes that prophets are infallible before and after prophethood, supported by miracles.
This is the sounder view according to the author.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The commentary from Sharh Aqidat al-Tahawi elaborates: a major sin can be 'downgraded' if accompanied by repentance, awe, etc., while a minor sin can be 'upgraded' if accompanied by indifference and contempt. This relativity is rooted in the heart's state, an extra dimension beyond the act itself. Finally, the author reaffirms that prophetic infallibility covers both before and after prophethood (the sounder view), and prophets are supported by miracles.

Key Terms

حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين
العصمة
المعجزات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The passage teaches that the same sin can be considered major or minor depending on the heart's state and the person's spiritual station.

Key Takeaway
A sin's gravity is relative to the heart's state and the person's station, and prophets are infallible.

?

?
Prophets are infallible; you are not. Yet you claim to follow them. How does your heart respond when you sin? Is it indifference or genuine brokenness? This very night, before you sleep, pick one sin you usually treat lightly and beg Allah for forgiveness with the shame this passage demands.

Two Definitions of Major Sin
Two definitions of major sin presented.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main scholarly definitions of a major sin (kabira) and why the author prefers the one based on mention of divine threat.

Core Idea
Major sins are defined either as all prohibitions or as sins threatened with punishment.

Before we can discuss the gravity of sins, we need a clear definition of what counts as a major sin. Early scholars differed: some considered every prohibited act as major, while others restricted it to sins accompanied by a divine threat in the Quran. This passage presents both views and identifies the stronger one.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Carefully read the first part of the source, from 'واختلفوا' to 'فتدبر'. Notice the two opinions and the Quranic evidence.
2
Identify the two definitions
Ibn Sirin defines kabira as every prohibition. Al-Hasan et al. define it as sins connected to wa'id. Understand each.
3
Analyse the preference
The author says 'وهذا هو الأظهر' (this is the more apparent view). Reflect on why the second view might be more precise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في حد الكبيرة، فقال ابن سيرين رضي الله عنه : كل ما نهى الله عنه فهو كبيرة، ويؤيده ظاهر قوله سبحانه وَإِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَابِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ الآية [النساء: ٣١]، وقال الحسن وسعيد بن جبير والضحاك وغيرهم ما جاء في القرآن مقرونا بذكر الوعيد فهو كبيرة، وهذا هو الأظهر فتدبر. ثم اعلم أن ترك الفرض أو الواجب ولو مرّة بلا عذر كبيرة وكذا ارتكاب الحرام. وترك السنة مرّة بلا عذر تساهلا وتكاسلا عنها صغيرة، وكذا ارتكاب الكراهة والإصرار على السنة أو ارتكاب الكراهة كبيرة، إلا أنها كبيرة دون كبيرة؛ لأن الكبير والصغير من الأمور الإضافية والأحوال النسبية ولذا قيل: حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين.
And they differed concerning the definition of the major sin (kabira). Ibn Sirin (may Allah be pleased with him) said: Everything that Allah has forbidden is a major sin, and this is supported by the apparent meaning of His statement: 'If you avoid the great sins (kaba'ir) which you are forbidden' [al-Nisa' 31]. And al-Hasan, Sa'id ibn Jubayr, al-Dahhak, and others said: Whatever occurs in the Qur'an connected with a threat (wa'id) is a major sin, and this is the more apparent view, so reflect. Then know that abandoning an obligatory (fard) or necessary (wajib) act, even once without excuse, is a major sin, and likewise committing a forbidden act. And abandoning a sunna once without excuse out of leniency and laziness is a minor sin, and likewise committing a disliked act (karaha). And persistence (israr) upon a sunna or committing a disliked act is a major sin, but it is a major sin less than other major sins; because the major and minor are among relative matters and relational states. Hence it is said: 'The good deeds of the righteous are the evil deeds of those brought near (al-muqarrabin).'
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage defines major sin (kabira) and minor sin (saghira), discussing the views of early scholars. It begins by noting the disagreement on the definition. Ibn Sirin holds that every prohibition by Allah is a major sin, citing Quran 4:31 as support. Al-Hasan, Sa'id ibn Jubayr, al-Dahhak, and others define a major sin as any sin mentioned in the Quran with a threat of punishment, which the author considers the stronger view. The passage then provides practical rulings: abandoning an obligatory or necessary act even once without excuse is a major sin, as is committing a forbidden act. Abandoning a sunna once without excuse due to laxity is a minor sin, and similarly committing a disliked act. However, persistence (israr) on a sunna or on a disliked act elevates it to a major sin, though lesser than other major sins. The author explains that 'major' and 'minor' are relative terms, depending on context, and cites the saying 'The good deeds of the righteous are the evil deeds of those brought near,' illustrating that even righteous acts can be considered minor relative to higher stations.

كبيرة
وعيد

Checkpoint

A) Ibn Sirin's definition
B) The definition of al-Hasan et al.
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Major sins are most precisely defined as sins mentioned in the Quran with a divine threat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the two definitions do you actually use when you judge your own sins? Do you treat every sin as a major offense, or do you downplay sins that lack an explicit threat? How does that affect your urgency to repent?
?

?
When you hear a Quranic verse mentioning a threat for a sin, does it stir fear in your heart? If not, what might be blocking that fear? What step will you take today to revive that awareness?

Rulings on Major and Minor Sins
Practical rulings on major/minor sins and their relativity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the practical criteria for classifying sins as major or minor, including the role of persistence and the relative nature of these categories.

Core Idea
Persistence turns a minor sin into a major sin, and sin categories are relative.

After defining major sin, the passage provides concrete rulings on what makes an act major or minor. It covers obligations, sunna, disliked acts, and introduces the crucial concept of persistence (israr). Finally, it explains that 'major' and 'minor' are relative terms, varying with the person's spiritual state.

Inner States and Sin Severity
Sharh al-Tahawiyya explains how inner states affect sin severity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how inner states such as modesty, fear, and deeming sin great can change the classification of a sin from major to minor and vice versa.

Core Idea
The heart's state can transform a sin's gravity more than the act itself.

This passage from the commentary on 'Aqida al-Tahawiyya reveals a profound dimension of Islamic ethics: sin is not merely about the external act but also about the internal disposition. The author explains that a major sin can be lightened if accompanied by humility, fear, and a sense of its gravity, while a minor sin can be magnified by carelessness and lack of modesty. This lesson explores these principles and their implications for understanding divine justice and mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the main argument without overanalyzing.
Notice the key terms: كبيرة, صغيرة, حياء, خوف, استعظام, استهانة.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two opposite cases: a major sin mitigated by inner states, and a minor sin aggravated by inner states. Identify the specific qualities mentioned.
The qualities for mitigation are modesty, fear, and deeming it great; for aggravation, lack of modesty, carelessness, lack of fear, and deeming it insignificant.
3
Reflect on the role of the heart
The author emphasizes that these inner states are a quality of the heart beyond the mere act. Consider why this distinction matters for accountability.
This is an additional dimension: the same external act can have different spiritual weight depending on the heart's condition.
4
Consider the forgiveness aspect
The passage ends with a note that a person of great goodness may be forgiven grave sins that others would not be forgiven. How does this relate to the previous points?
This highlights that one's overall record of goodness can influence divine leniency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال شارح عقيدة الطحاوي: ثم أمر ينبغي التفطن له وهو أن الكبيرة قد يقترن بها من الحياء والخوف والاستعظام لها ما يلحقها بالصغائر. وقد يقترن بالصغيرة من قلة الحياء وعدم المبالات وترك الخوف والاستهانة بها ما يلحقها بالكبائر، وهذا أمر مرجعه إلى ما يقوم بالقلب وهو قدر زائد على مجرد الفعل والإنسان يعرف ذلك من نفسه وغيره. وأيضاً فإنه قد يعفى لصاحب الإحسان العظيم ما لا يعفى لغيره من الذنب الجسيم.
The commentator of 'Aqida al-Tahawiyya said: Then there is a matter that should be paid attention to: that a major sin may be accompanied by modesty, fear, and deeming it great (isti'zam), which causes it to be attached to the minor sins. And a minor sin may be accompanied by lack of modesty, carelessness, lack of fear, and deeming it insignificant (istikhafa), which causes it to be attached to the major sins. This matter has its reference to what is established in the heart, which is an additional quality beyond the mere act, and a person knows this from himself and from others. Also, a person of great goodness may be forgiven what is not forgiven for others of grave sin.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage, quoting the commentator of 'Aqida al-Tahawiyya, discusses how subjective inner states can change the classification of sins. The author points out that a major sin can be mitigated to the level of a minor sin if accompanied by modesty, fear of Allah, and a sense of its gravity. Conversely, a minor sin can be aggravated to a major sin if accompanied by lack of modesty, carelessness, no fear, and treating it lightly. These factors reside in the heart (qalb) and are an additional dimension beyond the external act. The commentator adds that a person of great virtue may be forgiven a severe sin that would not be forgiven for others, implying that one's overall state of piety influences divine forgiveness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sin is only about the external action, not inner states.
Correction: Islam considers the heart's condition as a crucial factor; modesty, fear, and deeming sin great can reduce severity, while carelessness increases it.

The Best After the Prophets: Clarifying 'After' and Identifying Abu Bakr
Author clarifies 'after' and asserts Abu Bakr's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what 'after the Messenger of Allah' means and why Abu Bakr is considered the best of all people after the prophets.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr is the best after the prophets; temporal posteriority is meant.

Before establishing Abu Bakr's superiority, the author clarifies a crucial phrase: 'after the Messenger of Allah.' He explains that this refers to the time after the Prophet's existence, not a temporal gap after all prophets, since Jesus will descend later. This sets the stage for understanding Abu Bakr's unique status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'after'
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting how the author defines 'after' and mentions living prophets.
Pay attention to the phrase 'يأبى الله والمسلمون إلا أبا بكر' which appears later.
2
Understand the clarification
The author explains that 'after the Messenger of Allah' means after his existence. He also mentions that Jesus existed before but will descend after, and that four prophets are alive.
This clarifies that Abu Bakr's excellence is after all prophets, not just Muhammad.
3
Identify Abu Bakr's virtues
The passage then states that Abu Bakr is the best of people after the prophets, and explains his title 'al-Siddiq' and his lineage.
Reflect on why truthfulness and precedence make him the best.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفضل الناس بعد رسول الله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم أي بعد وجوده لأنه خاتم النبيين حال شهوده وأما عيسى فقد وجد قبله وإن كان يقع نزوله بعده، ولا يبعد أن يقال: اد الإمام الأعظم البعدية الزمانية؛ ففي شرح المقاصد: ذهب العظماء العلماء إلى أن أربعة من الأنبياء في زمرة الأحياء الخضر وإلياس في الأرض، وعيسى وإدريس في السماء. والحاصل أن أفضل الناس بعد الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام أبو بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه) كان اسمه في الجاهلية عبد الكعبة، فسماه رسول الله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم عبد الله واسم ابيه عائشة رضي الله بن سعد عامر بن كعب بن بن تيم بن مرة بن كعب ابن لؤي بن غالب بن ابو قحافة عثمان فهر القرشي التيمي، وهو الصديق لكثرة صدقه وتحقيقه وقوة تصديقه وسبق توفيقه، فهو أفضل الأولياء من الأولين والآخرين. وقد حكي الإجماع على ذلك، ولا عبرة بمخالفة الروافض هنالك، وقد استخلفه عليه الصلاة والسلام في الصلاة فكان هو الخليفة حقا وصدقا. وفي الصحيحين عن عنها أنها قالت: ((دخل علي رسول الله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم في اليوم الذي بدئ فيه فقال : ادعي إلي أباك وأخاك حتى أكتب لأبي بكر كتابا، ثم قال : يأبى الله والمسلمون إلا أبا بكر )) . (۱) وأما قول عمر: «إن أستخلف فقد استخلف من هو خير مني، يعني أبا بكر الله عنه، وإن لا أستخلف فلم يستخلف من هو خير مني، يعني النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم، فلعل مراده لم يستخلف بعهدِ مكتوب ولو كتب عهدا لكتبه لأبي بكر، بل قد أراد كتابته ثم تركه وقال: ((يأبى الله والمسلمون إلا أبا بكر)). (۲) فكان هذا أبلغ من مجرد العهد، فإنه دل المسلمين على استخلاف أبي بكر بالفعل والقول، واختاره لخلافته اختيار راض بذلك، وعزم على أن يكتب بذلك عهدا هنالك، ثم علم أن المسلمين يجتمعون عليه فترك الكتابة اكتفاء بإرادة الله تعالى واختيار الأمة، ثم عزم على ذلك في مرضه يوم الخميس، فلما حصل
And the best of people after the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, i.e., after his existence, because he is the seal of the prophets at the time of his witnessing. As for Jesus, he existed before him, although his descent will occur after him. And it is not far-fetched that the Great Imam meant temporal posteriority. In Sharh al-Maqasid: The great scholars went to the view that four of the prophets are in the company of the living: al-Khidr and Ilyas on earth, and Jesus and Idris in heaven. The conclusion is that the best of people after the prophets, peace be upon them, is Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, may Allah be pleased with him. His name in the pre-Islamic period was 'Abd al-Ka'ba; the Messenger of Allah named him 'Abd Allah. His father's name is Abu Quhafa 'Uthman, and his lineage: he is 'Amir ibn Ka'b ibn Sa'd ibn Tamm ibn Murra ibn Ka'b ibn Lu'ayy ibn Ghalib ibn Fihr al-Qurashi al-Taymi. And he is al-Siddiq due to the abundance of his truthfulness, verification, strength of his belief, and precedence of his success. So he is the best of the awliya from the first and the last. Consensus has been reported on that, and the opposition of the Rafidah is of no account. The Prophet, peace be upon him, appointed him as his successor in prayer, so he was the caliph truly and sincerely. In the two Sahihs, from 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) that she said: 'The Messenger of Allah entered upon me on the day his illness began and said: Call your father and your brother so that I may write a document for Abu Bakr. Then he said: Allah and the Muslims refuse except Abu Bakr.' As for the saying of 'Umar: 'If I appoint a successor, then I have succeeded one who is better than me, meaning Abu Bakr; and if I do not appoint a successor, then I have not succeeded one who is better than me, meaning the Prophet,' perhaps he meant he did not appoint a successor by a written covenant. Had he written a covenant, he would have written it for Abu Bakr. In fact, he intended to write it but then left it, saying: 'Allah and the Muslims refuse except Abu Bakr.' This was more effective than a mere covenant, for it indicated to the Muslims the appointment of Abu Bakr by deed and word, and he chose him for his caliphate willingly. He resolved to write a covenant for him then, but then knew that the Muslims would unite upon him, so he left the writing, sufficing with the will of Allah and the choice of the community. Then he resolved on that during his illness on Thursday, when it happened.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage establishes the superiority of Abu Bakr after the prophets. It first clarifies the meaning of 'after the Messenger of Allah' — i.e., after his existence, since he is the seal; Jesus existed before but will descend after. The author mentions that Imam Abu Hanifa meant temporal posteriority, and cites from Sharh al-Maqasid the view that four prophets are alive: Khidr and Ilyas on earth, Jesus and Idris in heaven. The conclusion is that the best after all prophets is Abu Bakr. His pre-Islamic name, his lineage, and his title 'al-Siddiq' are explained, indicating his truthfulness and precedence. Consensus is reported on his superiority, and the Rafidites' objection is dismissed. The evidence from the Prophet's action is given: he appointed Abu Bakr to lead prayer, and intended to write a covenant for him but then said 'Allah and the Muslims refuse except Abu Bakr.' The saying of 'Umar is interpreted to mean that the Prophet did not leave a written covenant, but his explicit statement and the Muslims' consensus were even stronger proof.

Key Terms
أفضل الناس
أبو بكر الصديق
الصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'after the Prophet' means after the line of prophets, including those still alive.
Correction: The passage clarifies it means after the Prophet's existence, and Jesus will descend later, so Abu Bakr is best after all prophets.

Evidence for Abu Bakr's Superiority: Consensus and Prophetic Indications
Author provides evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority: consensus, prophetic actions, and hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key evidences—consensus, the Prophet's actions, and the hadith—that establish Abu Bakr's superiority and caliphate.

Core Idea
The Prophet's words and actions, plus Muslim consensus, prove Abu Bakr is the best successor.

Having established Abu Bakr's virtue, the author now presents the evidence. He reports consensus, dismisses opposing sects, and recounts how the Prophet appointed Abu Bakr to lead prayer and intended to write a covenant. The famous hadith 'Allah and the Muslims refuse except Abu Bakr' is cited, along with Umar's testimony.

Guided Path
1
Read the evidence section
Read the Arabic passage from 'وقد حكي الإجماع على ذلك' to the end, noting the different pieces of evidence.
The passage also includes a refutation of the Rafidah.
2
Understand consensus and its value
The author states that consensus (ijma) has been reported on Abu Bakr's superiority and that the opposition of the Rafidah is insignificant.
Consensus is a strong proof in Sunni Islam.
3
Analyze the Prophet's actions
The Prophet appointed Abu Bakr to lead prayer, which indicates his leadership. Then he intended to write a document but instead said 'Allah and the Muslims refuse except Abu Bakr.'
This action and statement together are stronger than a written will.
4
Reflect on Umar's saying
Umar's statement shows that the Prophet did not leave a written covenant but indicated Abu Bakr in a way that was even more effective.
The author interprets this to mean the Prophet's indication was clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفضل الناس بعد رسول الله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم أي بعد وجوده لأنه خاتم النبيين حال شهوده وأما عيسى فقد وجد قبله وإن كان يقع نزوله بعده، ولا يبعد أن يقال: اد الإمام الأعظم البعدية الزمانية؛ ففي شرح المقاصد: ذهب العظماء العلماء إلى أن أربعة من الأنبياء في زمرة الأحياء الخضر وإلياس في الأرض، وعيسى وإدريس في السماء. والحاصل أن أفضل الناس بعد الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام أبو بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه) كان اسمه في الجاهلية عبد الكعبة، فسماه رسول الله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم عبد الله واسم ابيه عائشة رضي الله بن سعد عامر بن كعب بن بن تيم بن مرة بن كعب ابن لؤي بن غالب بن ابو قحافة عثمان فهر القرشي التيمي، وهو الصديق لكثرة صدقه وتحقيقه وقوة تصديقه وسبق توفيقه، فهو أفضل الأولياء من الأولين والآخرين. وقد حكي الإجماع على ذلك، ولا عبرة بمخالفة الروافض هنالك، وقد استخلفه عليه الصلاة والسلام في الصلاة فكان هو الخليفة حقا وصدقا. وفي الصحيحين عن عنها أنها قالت: ((دخل علي رسول الله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم في اليوم الذي بدئ فيه فقال : ادعي إلي أباك وأخاك حتى أكتب لأبي بكر كتابا، ثم قال : يأبى الله والمسلمون إلا أبا بكر )) . (۱) وأما قول عمر: «إن أستخلف فقد استخلف من هو خير مني، يعني أبا بكر الله عنه، وإن لا أستخلف فلم يستخلف من هو خير مني، يعني النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم، فلعل مراده لم يستخلف بعهدِ مكتوب ولو كتب عهدا لكتبه لأبي بكر، بل قد أراد كتابته ثم تركه وقال: ((يأبى الله والمسلمون إلا أبا بكر)). (۲) فكان هذا أبلغ من مجرد العهد، فإنه دل المسلمين على استخلاف أبي بكر بالفعل والقول، واختاره لخلافته اختيار راض بذلك، وعزم على أن يكتب بذلك عهدا هنالك، ثم علم أن المسلمين يجتمعون عليه فترك الكتابة اكتفاء بإرادة الله تعالى واختيار الأمة، ثم عزم على ذلك في مرضه يوم الخميس، فلما حصل
And the best of people after the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, i.e., after his existence, because he is the seal of the prophets at the time of his witnessing. As for Jesus, he existed before him, although his descent will occur after him. And it is not far-fetched that the Great Imam meant temporal posteriority. In Sharh al-Maqasid: The great scholars went to the view that four of the prophets are in the company of the living: al-Khidr and Ilyas on earth, and Jesus and Idris in heaven. The conclusion is that the best of people after the prophets, peace be upon them, is Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, may Allah be pleased with him. His name in the pre-Islamic period was 'Abd al-Ka'ba; the Messenger of Allah named him 'Abd Allah. His father's name is Abu Quhafa 'Uthman, and his lineage: he is 'Amir ibn Ka'b ibn Sa'd ibn Tamm ibn Murra ibn Ka'b ibn Lu'ayy ibn Ghalib ibn Fihr al-Qurashi al-Taymi. And he is al-Siddiq due to the abundance of his truthfulness, verification, strength of his belief, and precedence of his success. So he is the best of the awliya from the first and the last. Consensus has been reported on that, and the opposition of the Rafidah is of no account. The Prophet, peace be upon him, appointed him as his successor in prayer, so he was the caliph truly and sincerely. In the two Sahihs, from 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) that she said: 'The Messenger of Allah entered upon me on the day his illness began and said: Call your father and your brother so that I may write a document for Abu Bakr. Then he said: Allah and the Muslims refuse except Abu Bakr.' As for the saying of 'Umar: 'If I appoint a successor, then I have succeeded one who is better than me, meaning Abu Bakr; and if I do not appoint a successor, then I have not succeeded one who is better than me, meaning the Prophet,' perhaps he meant he did not appoint a successor by a written covenant. Had he written a covenant, he would have written it for Abu Bakr. In fact, he intended to write it but then left it, saying: 'Allah and the Muslims refuse except Abu Bakr.' This was more effective than a mere covenant, for it indicated to the Muslims the appointment of Abu Bakr by deed and word, and he chose him for his caliphate willingly. He resolved to write a covenant for him then, but then knew that the Muslims would unite upon him, so he left the writing, sufficing with the will of Allah and the choice of the community. Then he resolved on that during his illness on Thursday, when it happened.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage establishes the superiority of Abu Bakr after the prophets. It first clarifies the meaning of 'after the Messenger of Allah' — i.e., after his existence, since he is the seal; Jesus existed before but will descend after. The author mentions that Imam Abu Hanifa meant temporal posteriority, and cites from Sharh al-Maqasid the view that four prophets are alive: Khidr and Ilyas on earth, Jesus and Idris in heaven. The conclusion is that the best after all prophets is Abu Bakr. His pre-Islamic name, his lineage, and his title 'al-Siddiq' are explained, indicating his truthfulness and precedence. Consensus is reported on his superiority, and the Rafidites' objection is dismissed. The evidence from the Prophet's action is given: he appointed Abu Bakr to lead prayer, and intended to write a covenant for him but then said 'Allah and the Muslims refuse except Abu Bakr.' The saying of 'Umar is interpreted to mean that the Prophet did not leave a written covenant, but his explicit statement and the Muslims' consensus were even stronger proof.

Prohibition of Reviling Companions and Virtue of Abu Bakr
Commentary commands love for all Companions, prohibits reviling, and proves Abu Bakr's certain virtue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why loving all Companions is obligatory, why reviling them is prohibited, and why denying Abu Bakr's companionship specifically is an act of disbelief.

Core Idea
Loving the Companions is faith; reviling them is forbidden; denying Abu Bakr's companionship is disbelief.

This passage from the commentary establishes the proper attitude toward the Companions of the Prophet (peace be upon him). It commands love for all of them, prohibits reviling any, and provides scriptural evidence from the Quran and hadith. The text also highlights the unique status of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, showing that denial of his companionship is a matter of faith and disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Understand the command to love all Companions
Read the opening phrase 'we love them all' and the author's explanation that it means we do not revile any Companion.
This sets the foundation for the entire passage.
2
Examine the evidence from hadith and Quran
Note the two proofs: the prophetic hadith 'Do not revile my Companions' and the Quranic verse about the foremost, which includes the four caliphs.
The verse is definitive (qat'i) in proving their faith and rank.
3
Recognize the special ruling for Abu Bakr
Learn that by consensus, denying Abu Bakr's companionship is disbelief, unlike denying others, because of explicit texts about him.
This highlights his unique virtue.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'we love them all', meaning we love all the Companions and do not revile any. The verse definitively proves their faith and high rank. Therefore, anyone who belittles them or denies the companionship of Abu Bakr specifically is a disbeliever, because of the explicit texts about him.

The Principle of Not Excommunicating for Sin
Defines the Ahl al-Sunnah stance on excommunication and refutes sectarian views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunnah position that a Muslim is not excommunicated for sin unless he deems it lawful, and how this differs from the Khawarij and Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Sin does not remove faith unless one deems it lawful.

This lesson establishes the foundational Sunni doctrine on excommunication. The author states clearly that no sin, major or minor, can expel a person from faith as long as he does not consider it permissible. He then contrasts this with the extreme positions of the Khawarij and Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to get the full statement of the doctrine.
2
Identify the key condition
Notice the phrase 'if he does not deem it lawful'. This is the only condition for excommunication.
The author explains that deeming lawful contradicts definitive proof.
3
Contrast with Khawarij and Mu'tazila
The Khawarij excommunicate for major sins; the Mu'tazila remove the name of faith but do not call the person a disbeliever. See how the Sunni position is in between.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this principle matters for communal harmony and dealing with sinners among Muslims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا نكفر مسلما بذنب من الذنوب وإن كانت كبيرة إذا لم يستحلها، ولا نزيل عنه اسم الإيمان. ولا نكفر) بضم النون وكسر الفاء مخففا أو مشددا، أي لا ننسب إلى الكفر (مسلما بذنب من الذنوب، أي بارتكاب معصية وإن كانت كبيرة) أي كما يكفر الخوارج مرتكب الكبيرة (إذا لم يستحلها) أي لكن إذا لم يكن يعتقد حلها؛ لأن من استحل معصية قد ثبتت حرمتها بدليل قطعي فهو كافر. ولا نزيل عنه اسم الإيمان أي ولا نسقط عن المسلم بسبب ارتكاب كبيرة وصف الإيمان، كما يقوله المعتزلة حيث ذهبوا إلى أن مرتكب الكبيرة يخرج عن الإيمان ولا يدخل في الكفر فيثبتون المنزلة بين الكفر والإيمان مع اتفاقهم على أن صاحب الكبيرة مخلد في النار. وأما ما روي عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله أنه قال لجهم: «اخرج عني يا كافر». فمحمول على التشبيه. ثم في بسط الإمام الكلام على نفي تكفير أرباب الآثام من أهل القبلة ولو من أهل البدعة دلالة على أن سب الشيخين ليس بكفر كما صححه أبو الشكور السالمي في تمهيده، وذلك لعدم ثبوت مبناه وعدم تحقق معناه، فإن سب المسلم فسق كما في حديث ثابت، وحينئذ يستوي الشيخان وغيرهما في هذا الحكم؛ ولأنه لو فرض أن أحدا قتل الشيخين بل والختنين بوصف الجمع لا يخرج عن كونه مسلما عند أهل السنة والجماعة، ومن المعلوم أن السب
And we do not excommunicate a Muslim for any sin, even if it is major, as long as he does not deem it lawful, and we do not remove from him the name of faith. 'And we do not excommunicate' – with dammah on the noon and kasrah on the fa, light or heavy, i.e. we do not attribute to disbelief (a Muslim) for any sin, i.e. for committing a disobedience even if it is major (i.e. as the Khawarij excommunicate the committer of a major sin) (as long as he does not deem it lawful), i.e. but if he does not believe it to be lawful; because whoever deems lawful a disobedience whose prohibition is established by a definite proof is a disbeliever. 'And we do not remove from him the name of faith' – i.e. we do not remove from the Muslim, due to committing a major sin, the description of faith, as the Mu'tazila say, who held that the committer of a major sin leaves faith but does not enter disbelief, thus affirming the station between disbelief and faith, while they agree that the person of the major sin is eternally in the Fire. As for what is narrated from Abu Hanifa, may God have mercy on him, that he said to Jahm: 'Get away from me, O disbeliever,' it is interpreted as [calling him] a comparison. Then, in the Imam's expansion of the speech on negating excommunication of the people of sins from the qibla, even if from the people of innovation, there is an indication that insulting the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar) is not disbelief, as Abu al-Shukur al-Salimi affirmed in his Tamhid, and that is because its basis is not established and its meaning is not realized; for insulting a Muslim is sin, as in the confirmed hadith, and then the two shaykhs and others are equal in this ruling; and because if it were assumed that someone killed the two shaykhs, even the two grandsons (Hasan and Husayn) in a collective manner, he would not leave being a Muslim according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, and it is known that insulting...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal definition and refutation. It states the Ahl al-Sunnah position: we do not excommunicate a Muslim for any sin, major or minor, unless he deems it lawful (istihlal). The author explains that deeming a sin lawful is disbelief because it contradicts a definitive textual proof. Then he contrasts with the Mu'tazila, who hold an intermediate position where the grave sinner is not a believer but not a disbeliever, yet is still eternally punished. The author then addresses a report about Abu Hanifa calling Jahm a disbeliever, explaining it as a figurative comparison (tashbih) not actual excommunication. Finally, the author extends the principle: insulting the two shaykhs is not excommunication-worthy, because insulting any Muslim is a sin, not disbelief. Even hypothetical murder of the two shaykhs and the grandsons does not remove one from Islam, as long as the person does not deem it lawful. The passage ends mid-sentence on the point that insulting is known.

Key Terms
تكفير
كبيرة
استحلال
المنزلة بين المنزلتين
خوارج
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahl al-Sunnah believe that no sin can ever make a person a disbeliever.
Correction: Deeming a sin lawful (istihlal) based on a definitive proof makes one a disbeliever; the person who merely commits the sin without deeming it lawful remains a believer.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition does a Muslim become a disbeliever due to a sin?
A) If he commits a major sin.
B) If he commits any sin repeatedly.
C) If he deems the sin lawful (istihlal).
D) If he rejects the Companions.

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Sunnah hold that a Muslim's faith is not nullified by sin alone; only deeming the sin lawful constitutes disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does the phrase 'we do not remove from him the name of faith' challenge your inner attitude? Write one concrete way you will guard your tongue from declaring someone a disbeliever this week.
?

?
The Mu'tazila agreed that the grave sinner is eternally punished, yet they invented a middle status that is not from revelation. How does this warn you against adding to Allah's religion? Are you tempted to follow your intellect over clear texts in any matter? Commit to seeking knowledge from the sources alone.

Insulting the Two Shaykhs and Excommunication
Extends the non-excommunication principle to offenses against the Companions and explains a report from Abu Hanifa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that insulting or even killing the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar) does not remove a person from Islam according to Ahl al-Sunnah, and why the report of Abu Hanifa calling Jahm a disbeliever is interpreted figuratively.

Core Idea
Even insulting the Companions does not cause excommunication unless istihlal occurs.

This lesson extends the principle of non-excommunication to serious offenses against the Prophet's Companions. The author uses the example of insulting or killing the two shaykhs to show that such actions are sinful but not disbelief, as long as the perpetrator does not deem them lawful. The lesson also explains the report about Abu Hanifa's harsh words to Jahm as a comparative statement, not actual excommunication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about Abu Hanifa and the two shaykhs
Read the Arabic and translation to see how the author handles these sensitive issues.
2
Understand the figurative interpretation
The author says Abu Hanifa's 'disbeliever' was a comparison (tashbih), not literal excommunication.
This shows caution in takfir even in heated debates.
3
Examine the argument about insulting the shaykhs
Insulting any Muslim is a sin (fisq), but not disbelief. The two shaykhs are not exceptions.
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: even killing them does not cause disbelief.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this doctrine prevents extremism against those who commit grave sins against venerated figures.

ولا نكفر مسلما بذنب من الذنوب وإن كانت كبيرة إذا لم يستحلها، ولا نزيل عنه اسم الإيمان. ولا نكفر) بضم النون وكسر الفاء مخففا أو مشددا، أي لا ننسب إلى الكفر (مسلما بذنب من الذنوب، أي بارتكاب معصية وإن كانت كبيرة) أي كما يكفر الخوارج مرتكب الكبيرة (إذا لم يستحلها) أي لكن إذا لم يكن يعتقد حلها؛ لأن من استحل معصية قد ثبتت حرمتها بدليل قطعي فهو كافر. ولا نزيل عنه اسم الإيمان أي ولا نسقط عن المسلم بسبب ارتكاب كبيرة وصف الإيمان، كما يقوله المعتزلة حيث ذهبوا إلى أن مرتكب الكبيرة يخرج عن الإيمان ولا يدخل في الكفر فيثبتون المنزلة بين الكفر والإيمان مع اتفاقهم على أن صاحب الكبيرة مخلد في النار. وأما ما روي عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله أنه قال لجهم: «اخرج عني يا كافر». فمحمول على التشبيه. ثم في بسط الإمام الكلام على نفي تكفير أرباب الآثام من أهل القبلة ولو من أهل البدعة دلالة على أن سب الشيخين ليس بكفر كما صححه أبو الشكور السالمي في تمهيده، وذلك لعدم ثبوت مبناه وعدم تحقق معناه، فإن سب المسلم فسق كما في حديث ثابت، وحينئذ يستوي الشيخان وغيرهما في هذا الحكم؛ ولأنه لو فرض أن أحدا قتل الشيخين بل والختنين بوصف الجمع لا يخرج عن كونه مسلما عند أهل السنة والجماعة، ومن المعلوم أن السب
And we do not excommunicate a Muslim for any sin, even if it is major, as long as he does not deem it lawful, and we do not remove from him the name of faith. 'And we do not excommunicate' – with dammah on the noon and kasrah on the fa, light or heavy, i.e. we do not attribute to disbelief (a Muslim) for any sin, i.e. for committing a disobedience even if it is major (i.e. as the Khawarij excommunicate the committer of a major sin) (as long as he does not deem it lawful), i.e. but if he does not believe it to be lawful; because whoever deems lawful a disobedience whose prohibition is established by a definite proof is a disbeliever. 'And we do not remove from him the name of faith' – i.e. we do not remove from the Muslim, due to committing a major sin, the description of faith, as the Mu'tazila say, who held that the committer of a major sin leaves faith but does not enter disbelief, thus affirming the station between disbelief and faith, while they agree that the person of the major sin is eternally in the Fire. As for what is narrated from Abu Hanifa, may God have mercy on him, that he said to Jahm: 'Get away from me, O disbeliever,' it is interpreted as [calling him] a comparison. Then, in the Imam's expansion of the speech on negating excommunication of the people of sins from the qibla, even if from the people of innovation, there is an indication that insulting the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar) is not disbelief, as Abu al-Shukur al-Salimi affirmed in his Tamhid, and that is because its basis is not established and its meaning is not realized; for insulting a Muslim is sin, as in the confirmed hadith, and then the two shaykhs and others are equal in this ruling; and because if it were assumed that someone killed the two shaykhs, even the two grandsons (Hasan and Husayn) in a collective manner, he would not leave being a Muslim according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, and it is known that insulting...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal definition and refutation. It states the Ahl al-Sunnah position: we do not excommunicate a Muslim for any sin, major or minor, unless he deems it lawful (istihlal). The author explains that deeming a sin lawful is disbelief because it contradicts a definitive textual proof. Then he contrasts with the Mu'tazila, who hold an intermediate position where the grave sinner is not a believer but not a disbeliever, yet is still eternally punished. The author then addresses a report about Abu Hanifa calling Jahm a disbeliever, explaining it as a figurative comparison (tashbih) not actual excommunication. Finally, the author extends the principle: insulting the two shaykhs is not excommunication-worthy, because insulting any Muslim is a sin, not disbelief. Even hypothetical murder of the two shaykhs and the grandsons does not remove one from Islam, as long as the person does not deem it lawful. The passage ends mid-sentence on the point that insulting is known.

Key Terms

تكفير
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anyone who insults the two shaykhs becomes a disbeliever.
Correction: Insulting them is a grave sin but not disbelief, as long as the person does not deem it lawful. The sin is the same as insulting any other Muslim.

Checkpoint

A) He becomes a disbeliever.
B) He remains a Muslim according to Ahl al-Sunnah.
C) He is excommunicated but not a disbeliever.
D) His case is undecided.

Key Takeaway
Even the most heinous acts against the Prophet's Companions do not remove a person from the fold of Islam unless accompanied by istihlal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If you truly believed that even killing the Companions does not remove faith without istihlal, how would your response to such people change?
?
Are there people in your life whom you have written off as 'beyond the fold' because of their beliefs or actions? What would it cost you to treat them as Muslims who are sinning, rather than as disbelievers? Can you name one person you can start seeing through this lens from today?

No Takfīr for Major Sin Without Istiḥlāl
Explains the creedal statement on sin and disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that a Muslim is not declared a disbeliever for committing a major sin, but only if he deems that sin lawful (istiḥlāl).

Core Idea
A believer remains a believer even after sin; only deeming the sin lawful leads to disbelief.

This lesson explains the foundational creedal statement from Al-Fiqh al-Akbar regarding the status of a Muslim who commits a major sin. The commentator clarifies the wording and the crucial exception of istiḥlāl, rejecting the extreme positions of the Khawārij who declare the sinner a disbeliever regardless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the grammatical explanations provided by the commentator.
2
Identify the condition for takfīr
The passage states that a Muslim is not declared a disbeliever due to a sin, even a major one, unless he deems it lawful. Locate the phrase that expresses this condition.
The condition is 'إذا لم يستحلّها' – if he does not deem it lawful.
3
Reflect on the difference between sin and istiḥlāl
Consider why deeming a sin lawful leads to disbelief while committing it does not. The passage explains that istiḥlāl contradicts the certainty of divine prohibition.
This distinction is crucial for understanding Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولا نكفر) بضم النون وكسر الفاء مخففا أو مشددا، أي لا ننسب إلى الكفر (مسلما بذنب من الذنوب، أي بارتكاب معصية وإن كانت كبيرةً) أي كما يكفر الخوارج مرتكب الكبيرة إذا لم يستحلّها) أي لكن إذا لم يكن يعتقد حلها؛ لأن من استحل معصية قد ثبتت حرمتها بدليل قطعي فهو كافر ولا) نزيل عنه اسم (الإيمان أي ولا نسقط عن المسلم بسبب ارتكاب كبيرة وصف الإيمان، كما يقوله المعتزلة حيث ذهبوا إلى أن مرتكب الكبيرة يخرج عن الإيمان ولا يدخل في الكفر فيثبتون المنزلة بين الكفر والإيمان مع اتفاقهم على أن صاحب الكبيرة مخلد في النار. وأما ما روي عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله أنه قال لجهم: «اخرج عني يا كافر». فمحمول على التشبيه.
(And we do not declare [as disbeliever]) with a damma on the nūn and kasra on the fā', either lightened or emphasized, meaning we do not attribute disbelief (to a Muslim due to a sin from among sins, i.e., due to committing an act of disobedience even if it is a major sin) i.e., unlike the Khawārij who declare the perpetrator of a major sin a disbeliever (when he does not deem it lawful) i.e., provided that he does not believe it to be permissible; because whoever deems permissible an act of disobedience whose prohibition is established by a decisive proof is a disbeliever. And we do not remove from him the name of (faith), meaning we do not strip the Muslim of the description of faith because of committing a major sin, as the Mu'tazila say, who hold that the perpetrator of a major sin leaves faith and does not enter disbelief, thus affirming an intermediate state between faith and disbelief, despite their agreement that the perpetrator of a major sin is eternally in the Fire. As for what is related from Abū Ḥanīfa, may Allāh have mercy on him, that he said to Jahm: 'Get away from me, O disbeliever,' it is interpreted (as referring to resemblance).
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the creedal statement from Fiqh al-Akbar: 'We do not declare a Muslim a disbeliever due to a sin, even a major sin, unless he deems it lawful.' The commentator begins by explaining the grammatical construction and meaning. He then contrasts the Sunni position with that of the Khawārij, who declare the perpetrator of a major sin a disbeliever even without istiḥlāl, and the Mu'tazila, who hold that such a person exits faith without entering disbelief (the intermediate state). The commentator affirms that decisive proofs necessitate disbelief only if the sin is deemed lawful. Finally, he addresses a report about Abū Ḥanīfa calling Jahm a disbeliever, interpreting it as referring to resemblance or figurative usage, not a literal accusation of disbelief for a mere sin.

كبرية
استحلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that committing a major sin automatically makes a person a disbeliever.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only deeming the sin lawful (istiḥlāl) leads to disbelief, not the act itself.

Checkpoint

A) Whenever the sin is committed.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim remains a believer despite sin; only istiḥlāl (deeming a sin lawful) removes him from the fold of Islam.

Refuting Khawārij and Mu'tazila on the Major Sinner
Rejects deviant sects' positions on the major sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni rejection of both the Khawārij and Mu'tazila positions regarding the major sinner, and the correct interpretation of Ibn Ḥanīfa's statement about Jahm.

Core Idea
The Sunni creed upholds faith despite sin, avoiding extremes of disbelief or an intermediate state.

The commentator continues by contrasting the Sunni position with two deviant sects: the Khawārij who declare the sinner a disbeliever, and the Mu'tazila who say he exits faith without entering disbelief (an intermediate state). The passage also addresses a historical report about Abū Ḥanīfa calling Jahm a disbeliever, interpreting it figuratively.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Mu'tazila
Read the second part of the Arabic passage which discusses the Mu'tazila view and the interpretation of Abū Ḥanīfa's statement.
Notice how the commentator rejects the intermediate state.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila hold that the major sinner leaves faith but does not enter disbelief — an intermediate state between faith and disbelief. The commentator rejects this and affirms that the sinner remains a believer.
The Mu'tazila also believe the sinner is eternally in Hellfire, which the commentator mentions.
3
Interpret the report about Abū Ḥanīfa
The passage concludes with a report that Abū Ḥanīfa called Jahm a disbeliever. The commentator interprets this as referring to similarity (tashbīh) or figurative usage, not a literal accusation for a sin.
This shows that even great imams used harsh language against heretical beliefs, but it does not contradict the general rule.

Reviling the Two Shaykhs Is Not Disbelief
Proof that reviling Abu Bakr and Umar is not kufr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reviling Abu Bakr and Umar is considered transgression (fisq) but not disbelief, based on hadith and rational analogy.

Core Idea
Reviling the Companions is sinful, but it does not remove one from Islam.

This passage tackles a delicate issue: whether insulting the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar) constitutes disbelief. The author firmly argues it does not, grounding his case in a sound hadith that labels reviling a Muslim as fisq, and extends that ruling equally to all, including the revered Companions. The reasoning is strengthened by an analogy with killing, which also does not remove one from Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the author begins by citing an authority (Abu al-Shukur al-Salimi) to establish the main claim.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The passage gives two reasons: (1) the basis for declaring reviling as kufr is not established, (2) reviling a Muslim is fisq by a clear hadith. Find these in the text.
The second argument uses the hadith that reviling a Muslim is fisq. This applies equally to all.
3
Understand the analogy with killing
The author presents a stronger case: even killing the two shaykhs would not remove one from Islam. Since reviling is less severe, it cannot be kufr.
This is a logical argument from the lesser to the greater.
4
Note the exception and interpretation rule
If someone deems reviling or killing lawful (istihlal), they become a disbeliever. Also, any hadith seeming to contradict must be interpreted, just as the hadith about abandoning prayer is interpreted.
This safeguards against misinterpretation of primary texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم في بسط الإمام الكلام على نفي تكفير أرباب الآثام من أهل القبلة ولو من أهل البدعة دلالة على أن سب الشيخين ليس بكفر كما صححه أبو الشكور السالمي في تمهيده، وذلك لعدم ثبوت مبناه وعدم تحقق معناه، فإن سب المسلم فسق كما في حديث ثابت، وحينئذ يستوي الشيخان وغيرهما في هذا الحكم؛ ولأنه لو فرض أن أحدا قتل الشيخين بل والختنين بوصف الجمع لا يخرج عن كونه مسلما عند أهل السنة والجماعة، ومن المعلوم أن السب دون القتل، نعم لو استحل السب أو القتل فهو كافر لا محالة وعلى تقدير ثبوت الحديث فيجب أن يؤوّل كما أوّل حديث : من ترك صلاةً متعمدا فقد كفر). (والحاصل أن الفسق والعصيان لا يزيل الإيمان فيصير كافرا ولا واسطة، وكذا البدعة لا تزيل الإيمان والمعرفة كإنكار المعتزلة صفات الله تعالى وخلق أفعال العباد وجواز رؤيته سبحانه في المعاد؛ لأنه مبني على تأويل ولو كان على وجه الفساد إلا التجسيم وإنكار علم الله بالجزئيات، فإنه يكفر بهما بالإجماع من غير نزاع.
Then in the Imam's expansion of speech on negating the excommunication of the sinful people of the qibla, even if they are from the people of innovation, it indicates that reviling the two shaykhs is not disbelief, as Abū al-Shukūr al-Sālimī corrected in his Tamhīd. That is because its basis is not established and its meaning is not verified, for reviling a Muslim is transgression (fisq) as is established in a sound hadith. Thus the two shaykhs and others are equal in this ruling; because even if one supposes that someone killed the two shaykhs—nay, even the two caliphs in a collective sense—he would not depart from being a Muslim according to Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamā'a, and it is known that reviling is less than killing. Indeed, if he deemed reviling or killing lawful, then he is a disbeliever without doubt. And on the assumption that a hadith (regarding killing or reviling) is established, it must be interpreted just as the hadith 'Whoever intentionally leaves prayer has disbelieved' is interpreted. (The conclusion is that transgression and disobedience do not remove faith so that one becomes a disbeliever, nor is there an intermediate state. Likewise, innovation does not remove faith and knowledge, such as the Mu'tazila's denial of Allāh's attributes, His creation of acts of servants, and the permissibility of seeing Him in the Hereafter; because it is based on interpretation, even if it is erroneous. Except for anthropomorphism (tajsīm) and denial of Allāh's knowledge of particulars, for one becomes a disbeliever by these by consensus without dispute.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a proof that reviling the two shaykhs (Abū Bakr and 'Umar) does not constitute kufr. The commentator begins by citing an authority (Abū al-Shukūr al-Sālimī) and provides two arguments: first, the basis for declaring it kufr is not established; second, reviling a Muslim is only fisq (transgression) according to a hadith, and thus it applies equally to all, including the two shaykhs. To strengthen the point, he argues that even killing them would not remove one from Islam, so reviling (which is less) certainly does not. The only exception is if the reviler deems the act lawful (istiḥlāl). Then the commentator concludes with a general rule: sin and innovation do not remove faith, except for anthropomorphism (tajsīm) and denying God's knowledge of particulars, which are kufr by consensus. Note: The examples of Mu'tazila denials are given as cases of erroneous interpretation that do not necessitate takfīr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that reviling the Companions automatically makes one a disbeliever, regardless of whether one considers it lawful.
Correction: The passage clarifies that reviling is fisq, not kufr, unless accompanied by istihlal (deeming it lawful).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on someone who reviles Abu Bakr and Umar without deeming it lawful?
A) He becomes a disbeliever.
B) He is a transgressor (fasiq) but remains a Muslim.
C) He is in an intermediate state between faith and disbelief.
D) His ruling is the same as a murderer.

Key Takeaway
Reviling the two shaykhs is fisq, not kufr, unless one considers it lawful.
